<p><b>Aleppo</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/oʊ/ long 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">ﺣﻠﺐ</span>‎‎ / <a href="/wiki/ALA-LC" class="mw-redirect" title="ALA-LC">ALA-LC</a>: <i><span title="ar ALA transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Ḥalab</span></i>, <small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Arabic" title="Help:IPA for Arabic">[ˈħalab]</a></span>) is a city in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, serving as the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Aleppo_Governorate" title="Aleppo Governorate">Aleppo Governorate</a>, the most populous <a href="/wiki/Governorates_of_Syria" title="Governorates of Syria">Syrian governorate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UNDATA_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNDATA-7">[7]</a></sup> With an official population of 2,132,100 (2004 census), Aleppo was the largest Syrian city before the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>; however, now Aleppo is likely the second-largest city in Syria after the capital <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>.</p>
<p>Aleppo is an ancient city, and one of <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_by_time_of_continuous_habitation#Western" class="mw-redirect" title="List of cities by time of continuous habitation">the oldest continuously inhabited cities in the world</a>; it may have been inhabited since the 6th millennium BC.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Excavations at Tell as-Sawda and Tell al-Ansari, just south of the <a href="/wiki/Old_city_of_Aleppo" class="mw-redirect" title="Old city of Aleppo">old city of Aleppo</a>, show that the area was occupied by <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorites</a> since at least the latter part of the 3rd millennium BC;<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and this is also when Aleppo is first mentioned in <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> tablets unearthed in <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, in which it is a part of the Amorite state of <a href="/wiki/Yamhad" title="Yamhad">Yamhad</a>, and noted for its commercial and military proficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Such a long history is attributed to its strategic location as a trading center midway between the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and Mesopotamia (i.e. modern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>).</p>
<p>For centuries, Aleppo was the largest city in the <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">Syrian region</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>'s third-largest after <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_of_the_Ottoman_Empire_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_of_the_Ottoman_Empire-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Russell.2C_Alexander_.281794.29_The_natural_history_of_Aleppo_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russell.2C_Alexander_.281794.29_The_natural_history_of_Aleppo-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gaskin.2C_James_J._1846_Geography_and_sacred_history_of_Syria_p.33_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaskin.2C_James_J._1846_Geography_and_sacred_history_of_Syria_p.33-13">[13]</a></sup> It was also one of the largest cities in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> before the advent of the Syrian Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera.com_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera.com-16">[16]</a></sup> The city's significance in history has been its location at one end of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, which passed through central Asia and Mesopotamia. When the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> was inaugurated in 1869, trade was diverted to sea and Aleppo began its slow decline. At the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of the Ottoman Empire">fall of the Ottoman Empire</a> after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Aleppo ceded its northern hinterland to modern Turkey, as well as the important <a href="/wiki/Baghdad_Railway" title="Baghdad Railway">railway connecting it to Mosul</a>. In the 1940s, it lost its main access to the sea, <a href="/wiki/Antakya" title="Antakya">Antakya</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0skenderun" title="İskenderun">İskenderun</a>, also to Turkey. Finally, the isolation of Syria in the past few decades further exacerbated the situation. This decline may have helped to preserve the old city of Aleppo, its medieval architecture and traditional heritage. It won the title of the "Islamic Capital of Culture 2006", and has had a wave of successful restorations of its historic landmarks.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aleppo_(2012%E2%80%9316)" title="Battle of Aleppo (2012–16)">Battle of Aleppo</a> the city suffered massive destruction,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and has been the worst-hit city in the Syrian Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In December 2016, the Syrian government achieved full control of Aleppo following a successful offensive.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Modern-day English-speakers commonly refer to the city as <i>Aleppo.</i> It was known in antiquity as <i>Khalpe</i>, <i>Khalibon</i>, and to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> as <i>Beroea</i> (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Βέροια</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, and again during the <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon" title="French Mandate for Syria and the Lebanon">French Mandate for Syria and the Lebanon</a> of 1923–1946, the name <i>Alep</i> was used. <i>Aleppo</i> represents the Italianised version of this.</p>
<p>The original ancient name, <i><b>Halab</b></i>, has survived as the current Arabic name of the city. It is of obscure origin. Some have proposed that <i>halab</i> means "iron" or "copper" in <a href="/wiki/Amorite" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorite">Amorite</a> languages, since the area served as a major source of these metals in antiquity. However, according to the 20th-century historian sheikh Kamel al-Ghazzi and to the contemporary linguist priest Barsoum Ayyoub, the name <b>Halab</b> (and consequently <b>Aleppo</b>) derives from the Aramaic word <i><b>Halaba</b></i> which means "white", referring to the color of soil and marble abundant in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The modern-day Arabic nickname of the city, <b>ash-Shahbaa</b> (Arabic: الشهباء), which means "the white-colored," also allegedly derives from the famous white marble of Aleppo.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the 11th century it was common <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbinic</a> usage to apply the term "<a href="/wiki/Zobah" title="Zobah">Aram-Zobah</a>" to the area of Aleppo, and many <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Jews" title="Syrian Jews">Syrian Jews</a> continue to do so.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_City_of_Aleppo" title="Ancient City of Aleppo">Ancient City of Aleppo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Aleppo" title="Timeline of Aleppo">Timeline of Aleppo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Aleppo" title="List of rulers of Aleppo">List of rulers of Aleppo</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-history_and_pre-classical_era">Pre-history and pre-classical era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pre-history and pre-classical era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Armi_(Syria)" title="Armi (Syria)">Armi (Syria)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yamhad" title="Yamhad">Yamhad</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yamhad_dynasty" title="Yamhad dynasty">Yamhad dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Aleppo_from_Citadel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ancient_Aleppo_from_Citadel.jpg/220px-Ancient_Aleppo_from_Citadel.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ancient_Aleppo_from_Citadel.jpg/330px-Ancient_Aleppo_from_Citadel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ancient_Aleppo_from_Citadel.jpg/440px-Ancient_Aleppo_from_Citadel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1286" data-file-height="854" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Aleppo_from_Citadel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ancient_City_of_Aleppo" title="Ancient City of Aleppo">Ancient Aleppo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Aleppo has scarcely been touched by archaeologists, since the modern city occupies its ancient site. The site has been occupied from around 5000 BC, as shown by excavations in Tallet Alsauda.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aleppo appears in historical records as an important city much earlier than <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>. The first record of Aleppo comes from the third millennium BC, in the <a href="/wiki/Ebla_tablets" title="Ebla tablets">Ebla tablets</a> when Aleppo was referred to as <b>Ha-lam</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Some historians, such as <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Horowitz" title="Wayne Horowitz">Wayne Horowitz</a>, identify Aleppo with the capital of an independent kingdom closely related to <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Armi_(Syria)" title="Armi (Syria)">Armi</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> although this identification is contested. The main temple of the storm god <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a> was located on the citadel hill in the center of the city,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> when the city was known as the city of <b>Hadad</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Udgravning_(Citadellet_Aleppo).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Udgravning_%28Citadellet_Aleppo%29.jpg/220px-Udgravning_%28Citadellet_Aleppo%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Udgravning_%28Citadellet_Aleppo%29.jpg/330px-Udgravning_%28Citadellet_Aleppo%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Udgravning_%28Citadellet_Aleppo%29.jpg/440px-Udgravning_%28Citadellet_Aleppo%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1210" data-file-height="908" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Udgravning_(Citadellet_Aleppo).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hadad Temple Inside <a href="/wiki/Aleppo_Citadel" class="mw-redirect" title="Aleppo Citadel">Aleppo Citadel</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Akkad" title="Naram-Sin of Akkad">Naram-Sin of Akkad</a> mention his destruction of Ebla and <a href="/wiki/Armani_(kingdom)" title="Armani (kingdom)">Armani/Armanum</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> in the 23rd century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388-29">[29]</a></sup> but the identification of Armani in the inscription of Naram-Sim as Armi in the Eblaite tablets is heavily debated,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> as there was no Akkadian annexation of Ebla or northern Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/First_Babylonian_Dynasty" title="First Babylonian Dynasty">Old Babylonian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_Empire" title="Old Assyrian Empire">Old Assyrian Empire</a> period, Aleppo's name appears in its original form as Ḥalab (Ḥalba) for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388-29">[29]</a></sup> Aleppo was the capital of the important <a href="/wiki/Amorite" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorite">Amorite</a> dynasty of <a href="/wiki/Yamhad" title="Yamhad">Yamḥad</a>. The kingdom of Yamḥad (c. 1800–1525 BC), alternatively known as the 'land of Ḥalab,' was one of the most powerful in the Near East during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Yarim-Lim_I" title="Yarim-Lim I">Yarim-Lim I</a>, who formed an alliance with <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> against <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_I" title="Shamshi-Adad I">Shamshi-Adad I</a> of <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Yamḥad was devastated by the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mursilis_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Mursilis I">Mursilis I</a> in the 16th century BC. However, it soon resumed its leading role in the Levant when the Hittite power in the region waned due to internal strife.<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Taking advantage of the power vacuum in the region, <a href="/wiki/Parshatatar" title="Parshatatar">Parshatatar</a>, king of the <a href="/wiki/Hurrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurrian">Hurrian</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">Mitanni</a> instigated a rebellion that ended the life of Yamhad last king <a href="/wiki/Ilim-Ilimma_I" title="Ilim-Ilimma I">Ilim-Ilimma I</a> in c. 1525 BC,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Subsequently, Parshatatar conquered Aleppo and the city found itself on the frontline in the struggle between the Mitanni, the Hittites and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niqmepa,_King_of_Alalakh" title="Niqmepa, King of Alalakh">Niqmepa</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alalakh" title="Alalakh">Alalakh</a> who descends from the old Yamhadite kings controlled the city as a vassal to Mitanni and was attacked by <a href="/wiki/Tudhaliya_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudhaliya I">Tudhaliya I</a> of the Hittites as a retaliation for his alliance to Mitanni.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Later the Hittite king <a href="/wiki/Suppiluliumas_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Suppiluliumas I">Suppiluliumas I</a> permanently defeated Mitanni and conquered Aleppo in the 14th century BC, Suppiluliumas installed his son <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Aleppo#The_Hittite_Dynasty" title="List of rulers of Aleppo">Telepinus</a> as king and a dynasty of Suppiluliumas descendents ruled Aleppo until the <a href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Late Bronze Age collapse">Late Bronze Age collapse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aleppo had <a href="/wiki/Cult" title="Cult">cultic</a> importance to the Hittites for being the center of worship of the <a href="/wiki/Teshub" title="Teshub">Storm-God</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkins.2C_John_David_2000_p.388-29">[29]</a></sup> this religious importance continued after the collapse of the Hittite empire at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a> in the 12th century BC, when Aleppo became part of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Empire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bryce3_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bryce3-36">[36]</a></sup> whose king renovated the temple of Hadad which was discovered in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the beginning of the 1st millennium BC, Aleppo became part of the <a href="/wiki/Aramean" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramean">Aramean</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Bit_Agusi" title="Bit Agusi">Bit Agusi</a> (which had its capital at <a href="/wiki/Arpad_(Syria)" class="mw-redirect" title="Arpad (Syria)">Arpad</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Lipinsky.2C_Edward_2000._p._195_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lipinsky.2C_Edward_2000._p._195-38">[38]</a></sup> Bit Agusi along with Aleppo was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> In the 8th century BC and became part of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> until the late 7th century BC,<sup id="cite_ref-Healy.2C_Mark_1992_p._25_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Healy.2C_Mark_1992_p._25-39">[39]</a></sup> before passing through the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Babylonian">Neo-Babylonians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Achamenid" class="mw-redirect" title="Achamenid">Achamenid Persians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kipfer.2C_Barbara_Ann_2000_p._626_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kipfer.2C_Barbara_Ann_2000_p._626-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the beginning of the 1st millennium BC, Aleppo became part of the <a href="/wiki/Aramean" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramean">Aramean</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Bit_Agusi" title="Bit Agusi">Bit Agusi</a> (which had its capital at <a href="/wiki/Arpad_(Syria)" class="mw-redirect" title="Arpad (Syria)">Arpad</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Lipinsky.2C_Edward_2000._p._195_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lipinsky.2C_Edward_2000._p._195-38">[38]</a></sup> Bit Agusi along with Aleppo and the entirety of the Levant was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> between the 10th and 8th centuries BC and became part of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> (911–605 BC) until the end of the 7th century BC,<sup id="cite_ref-Healy.2C_Mark_1992_p._25_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Healy.2C_Mark_1992_p._25-39">[39]</a></sup> before passing briefly through the hands of the short lived <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a> and then the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Achaemenid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian Achaemenid Empire">Persian Achaemenid Empire</a> between 546 BC and 332 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Kipfer.2C_Barbara_Ann_2000_p._626_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kipfer.2C_Barbara_Ann_2000_p._626-40">[40]</a></sup> The region remained known as <a href="/wiki/Aramea" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramea">Aramea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eber_Nari" class="mw-redirect" title="Eber Nari">Eber Nari</a> throughout these periods.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity">Classical antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classical antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brad_Northern_Basilica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Brad_Northern_Basilica.jpg/220px-Brad_Northern_Basilica.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Brad_Northern_Basilica.jpg/330px-Brad_Northern_Basilica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Brad_Northern_Basilica.jpg/440px-Brad_Northern_Basilica.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brad_Northern_Basilica.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Maronite" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite">Maronite</a> basilica in <a href="/wiki/Barad,_Syria" title="Barad, Syria">Barad</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> took over the city in 333 BC. <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_Nicator" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucus Nicator">Seleucus Nicator</a> established a <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenic Republic">Hellenic</a> settlement in the site between 301 and 286 BC. He called it <i>Beroea</i> (Βέροια), after <a href="/wiki/Veria" title="Veria">Beroea</a> in <a href="/wiki/Macedon" class="mw-redirect" title="Macedon">Macedon</a>.</p>
<p>Northern Syria was the center of gravity of the Hellenistic colonizing activity, and therefore of Hellenistic culture in the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>. As did other Hellenized cities of the Seleucid kingdom, Beroea probably enjoyed a measure of local autonomy, with a local civic assembly or <i><a href="/wiki/Boule_(ancient_Greece)" title="Boule (ancient Greece)">boulē</a></i> composed of free Hellenes.<sup id="cite_ref-Phenix.2C_Robert_R._2008_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phenix.2C_Robert_R._2008-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beroea remained under Seleucid rule until 88 BC when Syria was occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Armenian people">Armenian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Tigranes_the_Great" title="Tigranes the Great">Tigranes the Great</a> and Beroea became part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman</a> victory over Tigranes, Syria was handed over to <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> in 64 BC, at which time they became a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Syria" title="Roman Syria">Roman province</a>. Rome's presence afforded relative stability in northern Syria for over three centuries. Although the province was administered by a <a href="/wiki/Legatus" title="Legatus">legate</a> from Rome, Rome did not impose its administrative organization on the Greek-speaking ruling class or <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a> speaking populace.<sup id="cite_ref-Phenix.2C_Robert_R._2008_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phenix.2C_Robert_R._2008-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Roman era saw an increase in the population of northern Syria that accelerated under the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantines</a> well into the 5th century. In <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, Beroea was the second largest Syrian city after <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, the capital of Syria and the third largest city in the Roman world. Archaeological evidence indicates a high population density for settlements between Antioch and Beroea right up to the 6th century. This agrarian landscape still holds the remains of large estate houses and churches such as the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Saint_Simeon_Stylites" title="Church of Saint Simeon Stylites">Church of Saint Simeon Stylites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Phenix.2C_Robert_R._2008_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phenix.2C_Robert_R._2008-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beroea is mentioned in <a href="/wiki/2_Maccabees" title="2 Maccabees">2 Macc.</a> 13:4.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecclesiastical_history">Ecclesiastical history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ecclesiastical history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Citadel_10_-_Mosque_of_Abraham.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Aleppo_Citadel_10_-_Mosque_of_Abraham.jpg/220px-Aleppo_Citadel_10_-_Mosque_of_Abraham.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Aleppo_Citadel_10_-_Mosque_of_Abraham.jpg/330px-Aleppo_Citadel_10_-_Mosque_of_Abraham.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Aleppo_Citadel_10_-_Mosque_of_Abraham.jpg/440px-Aleppo_Citadel_10_-_Mosque_of_Abraham.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Citadel_10_-_Mosque_of_Abraham.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Mosque of Abraham in the Citadel of Aleppo, originally built by the Byzantines as a church</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The names of several bishops of the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_see" title="Episcopal see">episcopal see</a> of Beroea, which was in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Syria_Prima" title="Syria Prima">Syria Prima</a>, are recorded in extant documents. The first whose name survives is that of Saint <a href="/wiki/Eustathius_of_Antioch" title="Eustathius of Antioch">Eustathius of Antioch</a>, who, after being bishop of Beroea, was transferred to the important <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_see" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan see">metropolitan see</a> of <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Antioch" title="Patriarch of Antioch">Antioch</a> shortly before the 325 <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a>. His successor in Beroea Cyrus was for his fidelity to the Nicene faith sent into exile by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantius_II" title="Constantius II">Constantius II</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Seleucia" title="Council of Seleucia">Council of Seleucia</a> of 359, called by Constantius, <a href="/wiki/Meletius_of_Antioch" title="Meletius of Antioch">Meletius of Antioch</a> was transferred from <a href="/wiki/Sivas" title="Sivas">Sebastea</a> to Beroea but in the following year was promoted to Antioch. His successor in Beroea, Anatolius, was at a council in Antioch in 363. Under the persecuting Emperor <a href="/wiki/Valens" title="Valens">Valens</a>, the bishop of Beroea was Theodotus, a friend of <a href="/wiki/Basil_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Basil the Great">Basil the Great</a>. He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Acacius_of_Beroea" title="Acacius of Beroea">Acacius of Beroea</a>, who governed the see for over 50 years and was at the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a> in 381 and the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a> in 431. In 438, he was succeeded by Theoctistus, who participated in the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> in 451 and was a signatory of the joint letter that the bishops of the province of Syria Prima sent in 458 to Emperor <a href="/wiki/Leo_I_the_Thracian" title="Leo I the Thracian">Leo I the Thracian</a> about the murder of <a href="/wiki/Proterius_of_Alexandria" title="Proterius of Alexandria">Proterius of Alexandria</a>. In 518 Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justin_I" title="Justin I">Justin I</a> exiled the bishop of Beroea Antoninus for rejecting the Council of Chalcedon. The last known bishop of the see is Megas, who was at a synod called by <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Menas_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarch Menas of Constantinople">Patriarch Menas of Constantinople</a> in 536.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> After the Arab conquest, Beroea ceased to be a residential bishopric, and is today listed by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Titular_see" title="Titular see">titular see</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Very few physical remains have been found from the Roman and Byzantine periods in the Citadel of Aleppo. The two mosques inside the Citadel are known to be converted from churches originally built by the Byzantines.<sup id="cite_ref-Julia1_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Julia1-45">[45]</a></sup> They were later converted into mosques by the <a href="/wiki/Mirdasid_dynasty" title="Mirdasid dynasty">Mirdasids</a> during the 11th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_period">Medieval period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Medieval period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bab_Qinnasrin2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bab_Qinnasrin2010.jpg/220px-Bab_Qinnasrin2010.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bab_Qinnasrin2010.jpg/330px-Bab_Qinnasrin2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Bab_Qinnasrin2010.jpg/440px-Bab_Qinnasrin2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="853" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bab_Qinnasrin2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The old walls of Aleppo and the <a href="/wiki/Bab_Qinnasrin" title="Bab Qinnasrin">Gate of Qinnasrin</a> restored in 1256 by <a href="/wiki/An-Nasir_Yusuf" title="An-Nasir Yusuf">An-Nasir Yusuf</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Persia">Sassanid Persians</a> <a href="/wiki/Byzantine-Sassanid_War_of_602-628" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine-Sassanid War of 602-628">invaded and controlled</a> Syria briefly in the early 7th century. Soon after Aleppo fell to <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquests">Muslims</a> under <a href="/wiki/Abu_Ubaidah_ibn_al_Jarrah" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Ubaidah ibn al Jarrah">Abu Ubaidah ibn al Jarrah</a> in 637. In 944, it became the seat of an independent Emirate under the <a href="/wiki/Hamdanid" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamdanid">Hamdanid</a> prince <a href="/wiki/Sayf_al-Daula" class="mw-redirect" title="Sayf al-Daula">Sayf al-Daula</a>, and enjoyed a period of great prosperity, being home to the great poet <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutanabbi" title="Al-Mutanabbi">al-Mutanabbi</a> and the philosopher and <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">al-Farabi</a>. The city was sacked by a resurgent Byzantine Empire in 962, while Byzantine forces occupied it briefly from 974 to 987. The city and its Emirate became an Imperial vassal from 969 until the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Seljuk_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine–Seljuk Wars">Byzantine–Seljuk Wars</a>. The city was besieged by the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusaders</a> in 1124, but was not conquered.</p>
<p>On 9 August 1138, <a href="/wiki/1138_Aleppo_earthquake" title="1138 Aleppo earthquake">a deadly earthquake</a> ravaged the city and the surrounding area. Although estimates from this time are very unreliable, it is believed that 230,000 people died, making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_earthquakes#Deadliest_earthquakes_on_record" class="mw-redirect" title="List of earthquakes">seventh deadliest</a> earthquake in recorded history.</p>
<p>In 1128 Aleppo became capital of the expanding <a href="/wiki/Zengid_dynasty" title="Zengid dynasty">Zengid dynasty</a>, which ultimately conquered Damascus in 1154. In 1183 Aleppo came under the control of <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a> and then the <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayyubid">Ayyubid</a> Dynasty. When the Ayyubids were toppled in Egypt by the <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluks</a>, the Ayyubid emir of Aleppo <a href="/wiki/An-Nasir_Yusuf" title="An-Nasir Yusuf">An-Nasir Yusuf</a> became sultan of the remaining part of the Ayyubid Empire. He ruled Syria from his seat in Aleppo until, on 24 January 1260,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Aleppo_(1260)" title="Siege of Aleppo (1260)">city was taken</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> under <a href="/wiki/Hulagu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hulagu">Hulagu</a> in alliance with their vassals the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">Frank</a> knights of the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="Bohemond VI">Bohemond VI</a> and his father-in-law the <a href="/wiki/Cilician_Armenia" class="mw-redirect" title="Cilician Armenia">Armenian</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Hetoum_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Hetoum I">Hetoum I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The city was poorly defended by <a href="/wiki/Turanshah" class="mw-redirect" title="Turanshah">Turanshah</a>, and as a result the walls fell after six days of bombardment, and the citadel fell four weeks later. The Muslim population was massacred and many Jews were also killed.<sup id="cite_ref-Shelemay1998_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shelemay1998-48">[48]</a></sup> The Christian population was spared. Turanshah was shown unusual respect by the Mongols, and was allowed to live because of his age and bravery. The city was then given to the former Emir of <a href="/wiki/Homs" title="Homs">Homs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ashraf" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Ashraf">al-Ashraf</a>, and a Mongol garrison was established in the city. Some of the spoils were also given to Hethoum I for his assistance in the attack. The Mongol Army then continued on to <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, which surrendered, and the Mongols entered the city on 1 March 1260.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Souk_Al_Zirb_Aleppo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Souk_Al_Zirb_Aleppo.jpg/220px-Souk_Al_Zirb_Aleppo.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Souk_Al_Zirb_Aleppo.jpg/330px-Souk_Al_Zirb_Aleppo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Souk_Al_Zirb_Aleppo.jpg/440px-Souk_Al_Zirb_Aleppo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Souk_Al_Zirb_Aleppo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Souq az-Zirb, where coins were struck during the Mamluk period</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 1260, the Egyptian Mamluks negotiated for a treaty with the Franks of Acre which allowed them to pass through Crusader territory unmolested, and engaged the Mongols at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">Battle of Ain Jalut</a> on 3 September 1260. The Mamluks won a decisive victory, killing the Mongols' Nestorian Christian general <a href="/wiki/Kitbuqa" title="Kitbuqa">Kitbuqa</a>, and five days later they had re-taken Damascus. Aleppo was recovered by the Muslims within a month, and a Mamluk governor placed to govern the city. Hulagu sent troops to try to recover Aleppo in December. They were able to massacre a large number of Muslims in retaliation for the death of Kitbuqa, but after a fortnight could make no other progress and had to retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Atroush_Mosque,_Aleppo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Al-Atroush_Mosque%2C_Aleppo.jpg/220px-Al-Atroush_Mosque%2C_Aleppo.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Al-Atroush_Mosque%2C_Aleppo.jpg/330px-Al-Atroush_Mosque%2C_Aleppo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Al-Atroush_Mosque%2C_Aleppo.jpg/440px-Al-Atroush_Mosque%2C_Aleppo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2716" data-file-height="2008" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Atroush_Mosque,_Aleppo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Al-Otrush_Mosque" title="Al-Otrush Mosque">Al-Otrush Mosque</a> of the Mamluk period</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Mamluk governor of the city became insubordinate to the central Mamluk authority in Cairo, and in Autumn 1261 the Mamluk leader <a href="/wiki/Baibars" title="Baibars">Baibars</a> sent an army to reclaim the city. In October 1271, the Mongols took the city again, attacking with 10,000 horsemen from <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, and defeating the <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkmen people">Turcoman</a> troops who were defending Aleppo. The Mamluk garrisons fled to <a href="/wiki/Hama" title="Hama">Hama</a>, until Baibars came north again with his main army, and the Mongols retreated.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 20 October 1280, the Mongols took the city again, pillaging the markets and burning the mosques. The Muslim inhabitants fled for Damascus, where the Mamluk leader <a href="/wiki/Qalawun" class="mw-redirect" title="Qalawun">Qalawun</a> assembled his forces. When his army advanced, the Mongols again retreated, back across the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>.</p>
<p>In 1400, the Mongol-Turkic leader <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Tamerlane</a> captured the city again from the Mamluks.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> He massacred many of the inhabitants, ordering the building of a tower of 20,000 skulls outside the city.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> After the withdrawal of the Mongols, all the Muslim population returned to Aleppo. On the other hand, Christians who left the city during the Mongol invasion, were unable to resettle back in their own quarter in the old town, a fact that led them to establish a new neighbourhood in 1420, built at the northern suburbs of Aleppo outside the city walls, to become known as <i>al-Jdeydeh</i> quarter ("new district" <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">جديدة</span>‎‎).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_era">Ottoman era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ottoman era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Khusruwiyah_mosque_and_Aleppo_view2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Khusruwiyah_mosque_and_Aleppo_view2.jpg/220px-Khusruwiyah_mosque_and_Aleppo_view2.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Khusruwiyah_mosque_and_Aleppo_view2.jpg/330px-Khusruwiyah_mosque_and_Aleppo_view2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Khusruwiyah_mosque_and_Aleppo_view2.jpg/440px-Khusruwiyah_mosque_and_Aleppo_view2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Khusruwiyah_mosque_and_Aleppo_view2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Khusruwiyah_Mosque" title="Khusruwiyah Mosque">Khusruwiyah Mosque</a> of the Ottoman period</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Aleppo became part of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1516, when the city had around 50,000 inhabitants, or 11,224 households according to an ottoman census.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> It was the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Aleppo_Eyalet" title="Aleppo Eyalet">Aleppo Eyalet</a>; the rest of what later became Syria was part of either the eyalets of Damascus, Tripoli, Sidon or Raqqa. Following the Ottoman provincial reform of 1864 Aleppo became the centre of the newly constituted Vilayet of Aleppo in 1866.</p>
<p>Thanks to its strategic geographic location on the trade route between <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and the east, Aleppo rose to high prominence in the Ottoman era, at one point being second only to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in the empire. By the middle of the 16th century, Aleppo had displaced <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> as the principal market for goods coming to the Mediterranean region from the east. This is reflected by the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Levant_Company_of_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Levant Company of London">Levant Company of London</a>, a joint-trading company founded in 1581 to monopolize England's trade with the Ottoman Empire, never attempted to settle a factor, or agent, in Damascus, despite having had permission to do so. Aleppo served as the company's headquarters until the late 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Masters_2009_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masters_2009-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Khan_Shuneh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Aleppo_Khan_Shuneh.jpg/220px-Aleppo_Khan_Shuneh.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Aleppo_Khan_Shuneh.jpg/330px-Aleppo_Khan_Shuneh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Aleppo_Khan_Shuneh.jpg/440px-Aleppo_Khan_Shuneh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Khan_Shuneh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Khan al-Shouneh of 1546</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As a result of the economic development, many European states had opened consulates in Aleppo during the 16th and the 17th centuries, such as the consulate of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a> in 1548, the consulate of France in 1562, the consulate of England in 1583 and the consulate of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> in 1613.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the prosperity Aleppo experienced in the 16th and 17th century started to fade as silk production in Iran went into decline with the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> in 1722. By mid-century, caravans were no longer bringing silk from Iran to Aleppo, and local Syrian production was insufficient for Europe's demand. European merchants left Aleppo and the city went into an economic decline that was not reversed until the mid-19th century when locally produced cotton and tobacco became the principal commodities of interest to the Europeans.<sup id="cite_ref-Masters_2009_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masters_2009-54">[54]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Halil_%C4%B0nalc%C4%B1k" title="Halil İnalcık">Halil İnalcık</a>, "Aleppo ... underwent its worst catastrophe with the wholesale destruction of its villages by <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> raiding in the later years of the century, creating a long-running <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> which by 1798 killed half of its inhabitants."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The economy of Aleppo was badly hit by the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> in 1869. This, in addition to political instability that followed the implementation of significant reforms in 1841 by the central government, contributed to Aleppo's decline and the rise of Damascus as a serious economic and political competitor with Aleppo.<sup id="cite_ref-Masters_2009_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masters_2009-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Jdeydeh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Aleppo_Jdeydeh.jpg/170px-Aleppo_Jdeydeh.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Aleppo_Jdeydeh.jpg/255px-Aleppo_Jdeydeh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Aleppo_Jdeydeh.jpg/340px-Aleppo_Jdeydeh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="922" data-file-height="1229" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Jdeydeh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Narrow alley at the <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenian</a> quarter in <a href="/wiki/Al-Jdayde" title="Al-Jdayde">Jdeydeh</a>, early 17th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reference is made to the city in 1606 in William Shakespeare's <i>Macbeth</i>. The witches torment the captain of the ship the <i>Tiger</i>, which was headed to Aleppo from England and endured a 567-day voyage before returning unsuccessfully to port. Reference is also made to the city in Shakespeare's <i>Othello</i> when Othello speaks his final words (ACT V, ii, 349f.): "Set you down this/And say besides that in Aleppo once,/Where a malignant and a turbanned Turk/Beat a Venetian and traduced the state,/I took by th' throat the circumcised dog/And smote him—thus!" (Arden Shakespeare Edition, 2004). The English naval chaplain <a href="/wiki/Henry_Teonge" title="Henry Teonge">Henry Teonge</a> describes in his diary a visit he paid to the city in 1675, when there was a colony of Western European merchants living there.</p>
<p>The city remained Ottoman until the empire's collapse, but was occasionally riven with internal feuds as well as attacks of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> from 1823. Around 20–25 percent of the population died of <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a> in 1827.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In 1850 a Muslim mob <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_Aleppo_(1850)" title="Massacre of Aleppo (1850)">attacked</a> Christian neighbourhoods, tens of Christians were killed and several churches looted. Though this event has been portrayed as driven by pure sectarian principles, Bruce Masters argues that such analysis of this period of violence is too shallow and neglects the tensions that existed among the population due to the commercial favor afforded to certain <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Syria" title="Christianity in Syria">Christian minorities</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a> Reforms during this time which played a large role in creating antagonism between previously cooperative groups of Muslim and Christians in the eastern quarters of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Janissary rebels installed their own government when the Ottoman governor fled. The Ottomans took over the city weeks later killing some 5,000.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> By 1901, the city's population was around 110,000.</p>
<p>At the end of World War I, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Treaty of Sèvres">Treaty of Sèvres</a> made most of the Province of Aleppo part of the newly established nation of <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, while Cilicia was promised by France to become an Armenian state. However, <a href="/wiki/Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" class="mw-redirect" title="Kemal Atatürk">Kemal Atatürk</a> annexed most of the Province of Aleppo as well as Cilicia to <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Turkish_war_of_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkish war of independence">War of Independence</a>. The Arab residents in the province (as well as the Kurds) supported the Turks in this war against the French, including the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Hananu_Revolt" title="Hananu Revolt">Hananu Revolt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Hananu" title="Ibrahim Hananu">Ibrahim Hananu</a>, who directly coordinated with Atatürk and received weaponry from him. The outcome, however, was disastrous for Aleppo, because as per the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne" title="Treaty of Lausanne">Treaty of Lausanne</a>, most of the Province of Aleppo was made part of Turkey with the exception of Aleppo and <a href="/wiki/Alexandretta" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexandretta">Alexandretta</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-atak5_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atak5-60">[60]</a></sup> thus, Aleppo was cut from its northern satellites and from the Anatolian cities beyond on which Aleppo depended heavily in commerce. Moreover, the <a href="/wiki/Sykes-Picot" class="mw-redirect" title="Sykes-Picot">Sykes-Picot</a> division of the Near East separated Aleppo from most of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, which also harmed the economy of Aleppo. The situation was exacerbated further in 1939 when Alexandretta was annexed to Turkey,<sup id="cite_ref-atak_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atak-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-atak3_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atak3-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-atak4_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atak4-63">[63]</a></sup> thus depriving Aleppo of its main port of <a href="/wiki/Iskenderun" class="mw-redirect" title="Iskenderun">Iskenderun</a> and leaving it in total isolation within Syria.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (June 2015)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_mandate">French mandate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: French mandate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/State_of_Aleppo" title="State of Aleppo">State of Aleppo</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:General_Gouraud_marching_in_Aleppo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/General_Gouraud_marching_in_Aleppo.jpg/220px-General_Gouraud_marching_in_Aleppo.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/General_Gouraud_marching_in_Aleppo.jpg/330px-General_Gouraud_marching_in_Aleppo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/General_Gouraud_marching_in_Aleppo.jpg/440px-General_Gouraud_marching_in_Aleppo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="469" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:General_Gouraud_marching_in_Aleppo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General <a href="/wiki/Henri_Gouraud_(French_Army_officer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Henri Gouraud (French Army officer)">Gouraud</a> crossing through al-Khandaq street on 13 September 1920</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/State_of_Aleppo" title="State of Aleppo">State of Aleppo</a> was declared by the French General <a href="/wiki/Henri_Gouraud_(French_Army_officer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Henri Gouraud (French Army officer)">Henri Gouraud</a> in September 1920 as part of a French scheme to make Syria easier to control by dividing it into several smaller states. France became more hostile to the idea of a united Syria after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Maysaloun" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Maysaloun">Battle of Maysaloun</a>.</p>
<p>By separating Aleppo from Damascus, Gouraud wanted to capitalize on a traditional state of competition between the two cities and turn it into political division. The people in Aleppo were unhappy with the fact that Damascus was chosen as capital for the new nation of Syria. Gouraud sensed this sentiment and tried to manipulate it by making Aleppo the capital of a large and wealthier state with which it would have been hard for Damascus to compete. The State of Aleppo as drawn by France contained most of the fertile area of Syria: the fertile countryside of Aleppo in addition to the entire fertile basin of river <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>. The state also had access to sea via the autonomous <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Alexandretta" title="Sanjak of Alexandretta">Sanjak of Alexandretta</a>. On the other hand, Damascus, which is basically an oasis on the fringes of the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Desert" title="Syrian Desert">Syrian Desert</a>, had neither enough fertile land nor access to sea. Basically, Gouraud wanted to lure Aleppo by giving it control over most of the agricultural and mineral wealth of Syria so that it would never want to unite with Damascus again.<sup id="cite_ref-M._Andrew_1981_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M._Andrew_1981-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fieldhouse.2C_David_Kenneth_2006_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fieldhouse.2C_David_Kenneth_2006-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Grand_Seray.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Aleppo_Grand_Seray.jpg/220px-Aleppo_Grand_Seray.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Aleppo_Grand_Seray.jpg/330px-Aleppo_Grand_Seray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Aleppo_Grand_Seray.jpg/440px-Aleppo_Grand_Seray.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Grand_Seray.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Grand_Serail_d%27Alep" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Serail d'Alep">Grand Serail d'Alep</a>, originally planned as the government building of the <a href="/wiki/State_of_Aleppo" title="State of Aleppo">State of Aleppo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The limited economic resources of the Syrian states made the option of completely independent states undesirable for France, because it threatened an opposite result: the states collapsing and being forced back into unity. This was why France proposed the idea of a Syrian federation that was realized in 1923. Initially, Gouraud envisioned the federation as encompassing all the states, even Lebanon. In the end however, only three states participated: Aleppo, <a href="/wiki/State_of_Damascus" title="State of Damascus">Damascus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Alawite_State" title="Alawite State">Alawite State</a>. The capital of the federation was Aleppo at first, but it was relocated to Damascus. The president of the federation was <a href="/wiki/Subhi_Barakat" class="mw-redirect" title="Subhi Barakat">Subhi Barakat</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>-born politician from Aleppo.</p>
<p>The federation ended in December 1924, when France merged Aleppo and Damascus into a single Syrian State and separated the Alawite State again. This action came after the federation decided to merge the three federated states into one and to take steps encouraging Syria's financial independence, steps which France viewed as too much.<sup id="cite_ref-M._Andrew_1981_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M._Andrew_1981-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fieldhouse.2C_David_Kenneth_2006_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fieldhouse.2C_David_Kenneth_2006-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/Great_Syrian_Revolt" title="Great Syrian Revolt">Syrian Revolt</a> erupted in southern Syria in 1925, the French held in Aleppo State new elections that were supposed to lead to the breaking of the union with Damascus and restore the independence of Aleppo State. The French were driven to believe by pro-French Aleppine politicians that the people in Aleppo were supportive of such a scheme. After the new council was elected, however, it surprisingly voted to keep the union with Damascus. <a href="/wiki/Syrian_National_Block" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian National Block">Syrian nationalists</a> had waged a massive anti-secession public campaign that vigorously mobilized the people against the secession plan, thus leaving the pro-French politicians no choice but to support the union. The result was a big embarrassment for France, which wanted the secession of Aleppo to be a punitive measure against Damascus, which had participated in the Syrian Revolt. This was the last time that independence was proposed for Aleppo.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-independence">Post-independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Post-independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Quwwatli.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Aleppo_Quwwatli.jpg/220px-Aleppo_Quwwatli.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Aleppo_Quwwatli.jpg/330px-Aleppo_Quwwatli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Aleppo_Quwwatli.jpg/440px-Aleppo_Quwwatli.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Quwwatli.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Shukri_al-Quwatli" title="Shukri al-Quwatli">Shukri al-Quwatli</a> street, was known as Rue de France during the French mandate</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The period immediately following independence from France was marked by increasing rivalry between Aleppo and Damascus.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Aleppo feverishly called for an immediate union between Syria and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Hashimite Iraq</a>, a demand that was firmly rejected by Damascus. Instead, Damascus favoured a pro-Egyptian, pro-Saudi orientation and actively participated in the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in 1944, an organization that was seen by many <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab nationalist">Arab nationalists</a> as a 'conspiracy' aimed against the unification of the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Hashimite" class="mw-redirect" title="Hashimite">Hashimites</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The increasing disagreements between Aleppo and Damascus led eventually to the split of the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_National_Block" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian National Block">National Block</a> into two factions: the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(Syria)" title="National Party (Syria)">National Party</a>, established in Damascus in 1946, and the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Party_(Syria)" title="People's Party (Syria)">People's Party</a>, established in Aleppo in 1948 by Rushdi Kikhya and <a href="/wiki/Nazim_Qudsi" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazim Qudsi">Nazim Qudsi</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> An underlying cause of the disagreement, in addition to the union with Iraq, was Aleppo's intention to relocate the capital from Damascus. The issue of the capital became an open debate matter in 1950 when the Popular Party presented a constitution draft that called Damascus a "temporary capital."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Night_by_Charles_Hajj.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Aleppo_Night_by_Charles_Hajj.jpg/220px-Aleppo_Night_by_Charles_Hajj.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Aleppo_Night_by_Charles_Hajj.jpg/330px-Aleppo_Night_by_Charles_Hajj.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Aleppo_Night_by_Charles_Hajj.jpg/440px-Aleppo_Night_by_Charles_Hajj.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1063" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo_Night_by_Charles_Hajj.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bab_al-Faraj_Clock_Tower" title="Bab al-Faraj Clock Tower">Bab al-Faraj Clock Tower</a> with the Aleppo City Council tower in the left background</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a> in modern Syrian history was carried out in March 1949 by an army officer from Aleppo, <a href="/wiki/Hussni_Zaim" class="mw-redirect" title="Hussni Zaim">Hussni Zaim</a>. However, lured by the absolute power he enjoyed as a dictator, Zaim soon developed a pro-Egyptian, pro-Western orientation and abandoned the cause of union with Iraq. This incited a second coup only four months after his.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The second coup, led by <a href="/wiki/Sami_Hinnawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami Hinnawi">Sami Hinnawi</a> (also from Aleppo), empowered the Popular Party and actively sought to realize the union with Iraq. The news of an imminent union with Iraq incited a third coup the same year: in December 1949, <a href="/wiki/Adib_Shishakli" title="Adib Shishakli">Adib Shishakly</a> led a coup preempting a union with Iraq that was about to be declared.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo,_tilel_street.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Aleppo%2C_tilel_street.jpg/170px-Aleppo%2C_tilel_street.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Aleppo%2C_tilel_street.jpg/255px-Aleppo%2C_tilel_street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Aleppo%2C_tilel_street.jpg/340px-Aleppo%2C_tilel_street.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aleppo,_tilel_street.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tilel street</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Soon after Shishakly's domination ended in 1954, a <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Republic" title="United Arab Republic">union</a> with Egypt under <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdul_Nasser" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamal Abdul Nasser">Gamal Abdul Nasser</a> was implemented in 1958. The union, however, collapsed only two years later when a junta of young Damascene officers carried out a separatist coup. Aleppo resisted the separatist coup, but eventually it had no choice but to recognize the new government.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In March 1963 a coalition of <a href="/wiki/Baathist" class="mw-redirect" title="Baathist">Baathists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasserist" class="mw-redirect" title="Nasserist">Nasserists</a>, and Socialists launched a new coup whose declared objective was to restore the union with Egypt. However, the new government only restored the flag of the union. Soon thereafter disagreement between the Baathists and the Nasserists over the restoration of the union became a crisis, and the Baathists ousted the Nasserists from power. The Nasserists, most of whom were from the Aleppine middle class, responded with an insurgency in Aleppo in July 1963.</p>
<p>Again, the Ba'ath government tried to absorb the dissent of the Syrian middle class (whose center of political activism was Aleppo) by putting to the front <a href="/wiki/Amin_al-Hafiz" title="Amin al-Hafiz">Amin al-Hafiz</a>, a Baathist military officer from Aleppo.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/Hafez_al-Assad" title="Hafez al-Assad">Hafez al-Assad</a>, who came to power in 1970, relied on support from the business class in Damascus.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> This gave Damascus further advantage over Aleppo, and hence Damascus came to dominate the Syrian economy. The strict centralization of the Syrian state, the intentional direction of resources towards Damascus, and the hegemony Damascus enjoys over the Syrian economy made it increasingly hard for Aleppo to compete. Hence, Aleppo is no longer an economic or cultural capital of Syria as it once used to be.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, Aleppo was named by the Islamic Educational Scientific and Cultural Organization (ISESCO) as the capital of Islamic culture.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syrian_Civil_War">Syrian Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aleppo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Syrian Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian civil war">Syrian civil war</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aleppo_(2012%E2%80%932016)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Aleppo (2012–2016)">Battle of Aleppo (2012–2016)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saadallah_after_the_explosion.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saadallah_after_the_explosion.jpg/220px-Saadallah_after_the_explosion.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saadallah_after_the_explosion.jpg/330px-Saadallah_after_the_explosion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saadallah_after_the_explosion.jpg/440px-Saadallah_after_the_explosion.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1152" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saadallah_after_the_explosion.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The scene at <a href="/wiki/Saadallah_Al-Jabiri_Square" class="mw-redirect" title="Saadallah Al-Jabiri Square">Saadallah Al-Jabiri Square</a> after being targeted by the armed opposition in October 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 12 August 2011, some months after protests had begun elsewhere in Syria, anti-government protests were held in several districts of Aleppo, including the city's Sakhour district. At least two protesters had been shot dead by security forces during a demonstration in Sakhour with tens of thousands attendees.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Two months later a pro-government demonstration was held in <a href="/wiki/Saadallah_Al-Jabiri_Square" class="mw-redirect" title="Saadallah Al-Jabiri Square">Saadallah Al-Jabiri Square</a>. According to the <i>New York Times</i>, the 11 October 2011 rally in support of <a href="/wiki/President_of_Syria" title="President of Syria">president</a> <a href="/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad" title="Bashar al-Assad">Bashar al-Assad</a> was held by large crowds,<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-71">[71]</a></sup> while state and local media claimed more than 1.5 million attended and that it was one of the largest rallies ever held in Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 2012 security forces began to be targeted with bombings. On <a href="/wiki/2012_Aleppo_bombings" class="mw-redirect" title="2012 Aleppo bombings">10 February 2012</a>, suicide <a href="/wiki/Car_bombs" class="mw-redirect" title="Car bombs">car bombs</a> exploded outside two security compounds – the Military Intelligence Directorate's local headquarters, and a Security Preservation forces barracks<sup id="cite_ref-herald-sun_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-herald-sun-73">[73]</a></sup> – reportedly killing 28 (four civilians, thirteen military personnel and eleven security personnel<sup id="cite_ref-herald-sun_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-herald-sun-73">[73]</a></sup>) and wounding 235.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-74">[74]</a></sup> On 18 March 2012, another car bomb blast in a residential neighbourhood reportedly killed two security personnel and one female civilian, and wounded 30 residents.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late July 2012, the conflict reached Aleppo in earnest when fighters from the surrounding countryside mounted their first offensive there,<sup id="cite_ref-mogelson_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mogelson-77">[77]</a></sup> apparently trying to capitalise on momentum gained during the Damascus assault.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Since then some of the civil war's "most devastating bombing and fiercest fighting has taken place" in Aleppo, often in residential areas.<sup id="cite_ref-mogelson_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mogelson-77">[77]</a></sup> In the summer, autumn and winter of 2012 house-to-house fighting between armed opposition and government forces has continued, and as of spring 2013 the Syrian army has entrenched itself in the western part of Aleppo (government forces were operating from a military base in the southern part of the city) and the armed opposition in the eastern part with a <a href="/wiki/No_man%27s_land" title="No man's land">no man's land</a> between them.<sup id="cite_ref-mogelson_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mogelson-77">[77]</a></sup> One estimate of casualties by an international humanitarian organizations is 13,500 killed – 1,500 under 5 years of age – and 23,000 injured.<sup id="cite_ref-mogelson_77-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mogelson-77">[77]</a></sup> Local police stations in the city were a focus of conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT73112_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT73112-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT8312_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT8312-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Syrian_opposition" title="Syrian opposition">armed opposition</a> has reportedly rounded up and executed prominent supporters of Bashar al-Assad and pro-<a href="/wiki/Arab_Socialist_Ba%27ath_Party_%E2%80%93_Syria_Region" title="Arab Socialist Ba'ath Party – Syria Region">Ba'ath</a> activists.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> A series of car bomb attacks and extensive looting have been attributed to opposition forces as well.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lost_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lost-85">[85]</a></sup> Contested and rebel-held parts of the city have been subject to "continuous jet, mortar, and artillery bombardment" by pro-government forces.<sup id="cite_ref-mogelson_77-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mogelson-77">[77]</a></sup> Analysis of satellite imagery has shown the destruction as "severely lopsided", with opposition-controlled neighbourhoods overwhelmingly hit. "Before and after" photographs from Aleppo neighbourhoods of Ard al-Hamra, Tariq al-Bab, and Jabal Badro have shown the destruction where missiles have slammed into residential areas.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of the severe battle, many sections in <a href="/wiki/Al-Madina_Souq" title="Al-Madina Souq">Al-Madina Souq</a> (part of the <a href="/wiki/Old_City_of_Aleppo" class="mw-redirect" title="Old City of Aleppo">Old City of Aleppo</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>), including parts of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Aleppo" title="Great Mosque of Aleppo">Great Mosque of Aleppo</a> and other medieval buildings in the ancient city, were destroyed and ruined or burnt in late summer 2012 as the armed groups of the <a href="/wiki/Free_Syrian_Army" title="Free Syrian Army">Free Syrian Army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Arab_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian Arab Army">Syrian Arab Army</a> <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aleppo_(2012)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Aleppo (2012)">fought</a> for control of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the Syrian Foreign Ministry, some 1,000 factories in Aleppo have been plundered, and their stolen goods transferred to Turkey with the full knowledge and facilitation of the Turkish government.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of November 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> journalist <a href="/wiki/Francesca_Borri" title="Francesca Borri">Francesca Borri</a> reported <a href="/wiki/Islamist" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamist">Islamist</a> fighters dominating rebel areas in Aleppo, and claimed they were focusing on enforcing <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a> law and fighting one another rather than the government. Masquerading as a Syrian traveling through the city, Borri reports widespread destruction, and evidence of malnutrition and disease.<sup id="cite_ref-Borri_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borri-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
